Summary of Main Findings

Prior Experience as a Hiring Criterion We asked the libraries in the sample to rank the following characteristics as a criterion for hiring a cataloger. We gave them four choices: not so important, somewhat important, important and very important. And for the criteria on evidence and leadership in a library setting, only 5.48% consider this very important. All the libraries that consider this criterion very important had more than 3,000 total students. Prior cataloging experience was considered very important by 27% of the survey participants and important by nearly 41% of the survey participants. Prior experience with cataloging technologies was considered very important by 13.5% of the colleges in the sample, while more than 43% considered it important. Research universities were somewhat more likely than others to consider a background in emerging cataloging technologies to be important; a third of them in the sample considered it very important, while another 44.44% considered it important. Experience with OCLC was considered very important by a shade more than a third of the libraries in the sample and important by 37.84%. The higher college attendance rose, the greater the percentage of libraries that thought of OCLC experience as a very important criterion for hiring a cataloger. Interestingly, it was the M.A./PhD granting institutions that were not full research universities that valued OCLC experience the most; half of them thought of it as an important criterion for hiring a cataloger. Close to 30% of the colleges in the sample believed that experience with the classification system in use was a very important criterion for hiring a cataloger, while 36.5% thought of this as an important criterion. Close to 42% of the colleges in the sample felt that experience with an integrated online system was an important criterion for hiring a cataloger. More public than private colleges thought of this as an important criterion, and the largest colleges in terms of total FTE attendance were also more likely than others to think highly of this characteristic. Nearly 56% of the research universities in the sample thought of experience with an integrated online system as an important factor in hiring a cataloger. Demonstration of teamwork was thought very important by close to 42% of participants, with private colleges far more likely than public ones to think of this as very important. Small, M.A./Ph.D. granting private colleges were particularly likely to value this characteristic in a cataloger. Perhaps collegiality is simply more valued or considered more important in these types of institutions. Authority control experience was considered a very important criterion for hiring by only 8.11% of survey participants, while a bit more than 35% considered it important. 21.62% considered authority control experience not so important as a hiring criterion. Far more public than public

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

40

colleges thought of it as an important criterion; more than 23.5% of colleges with more than 10,000 students thought of it as such. Experience with the formulation of policies and procedures was considered a very important criterion in hiring a cataloger for only 6.76% of the colleges in the sample, mostly by public colleges with more than 3,000 students. Experience with the subject thesaurus system in use was considered very important by 16.22% of survey participants, once again, mostly by public colleges and by colleges with more than 10,000 students. Training or teaching experience was considered very important only by 8.11% of those surveyed, mostly by community colleges. Half of the colleges in the sample considered good verbal communication to be an important criterion in hiring, while close to 38% considered it important. Experience with MARC standards was considered very important by more than 43% of those surveyed; more than 70% of colleges with more than 10,000 students considered MARC standards experience to be an important consideration in hiring. AACR2 experience was considered an important criterion in hiring for more than a third of participants and a very important criterion for 44.6%. Strong interpersonal and collaborative skills was considered an important criterion in hiring by 44.6% of participants; it was considered very important by more than 43% of them. This trait was particularly valued by colleges with fewer than 1,500 students. Experience with or knowledge of metadata standards such as Dublin Core was very important as a hiring criterion for only 1.35% of participants and important to only 20.2% of them. All of those who considered it very important were large public colleges with more than 10,000 students that were research universities. Good writing skills was considered an important criterion in hiring by 19.2% of participants and important by 53.42% of them.

Use of Professional and Paraprofessional Staff for Various Cataloging-Related Tasks About 27.3% of the survey participants routinely use paraprofessional staff for original cataloging. Public colleges were more than three times more likely than private colleges to use paraprofessionals for original cataloging, and larger colleges were more than twice as likely as smaller ones to do so. More than two-thirds of research universities use paraprofessionals for original cataloging. More than 83% of the colleges in the sample use professional librarians for original cataloging, including nearly 95% of the private colleges in the sample. The larger the college in terms of attendance, the less likely it was to use professional librarians for original cataloging. 76.6% of libraries in the sample routinely use paraprofessional support staff for copy cataloging.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

41

Less than 47% of colleges with fewer than 1,500 students use paraprofessional support staff for copy cataloging. About 52% of those sampled use professional librarians for copy cataloging. Only 39% of libraries in colleges with more than 10,000 students use professional librarians routinely for copy cataloging, as did only about 53% of community colleges and only 11.1% of research level universities. 6.49% of the libraries in the sample routinely use paraprofessional support staff for name authority cooperative work (NACO). All of these were public college libraries, mostly larger colleges. 41.56% of libraries in the sample use professional librarians for NACO work, as did close to 78% of research university libraries in the sample. 2.6% of the libraries in the sample use paraprofessional support staff for subject authority cooperative work (SACO). All of them were public colleges with between 3,000 and 10,000 students and all were community colleges. 27.3% of the libraries in the sample use professional librarians for SACO work; professional librarians from small colleges were more likely than those from larger colleges to do SACO work. About 19.5% of the libraries in the sample use paraprofessional support staff for master bibliographic record enhancement in OCLC. Use of paraprofessional support staff for this purpose was much more likely by public than private colleges by a factor of more than 4:1. More than 44% of colleges with more than 10,000 students use paraprofessional support staff for master bibliographic record enhancement, as do two-thirds of all research universities in the sample. More than 48% of libraries in the sample use professional librarians for master bibliographic record enhancement in OCLC. No libraries in the sample use support staff for participation in PCC, CONSERV or BIBCO 1 bibliographic work; 11.7% of libraries sampled use professional librarians for this purpose. Nearly all of these were private libraries. About 24.7% of the libraries in the sample use paraprofessional support staff for master bibliographic record enrichment in OCLC. Most of those doing so were public colleges and offered beyond the B.A. degree. Close to 56% used professional librarians for master bibliographic record enrichment in OCLC. 36.5% of the libraries sampled use paraprofessional support staff for subject analysis and subject heading; half of all public college libraries in the sample do so. 36.4% of colleges sampled use professional librarians for subject analysis and subject heading application, including 50% of public colleges in the sample. About 39% of the colleges in the sample routinely use paraprofessional support staff for classification, including half of all public colleges and half of all those with more than 10,000 students.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

42

More than 83% of libraries in the sample routinely use professional librarians for classification; all research libraries in the sample do so. 19.48% of the libraries sampled use paraprofessional support staff for master bibliographic record upgrades in OCLC, including close to 29% of public colleges. More than 57% routinely use professional librarians for this purpose. Close to 17% of the libraries in the sample routinely use paraprofessional support staff for establishment of local series, uniform title headings and authority records. Public colleges did so far more frequently than private colleges. A shade less than two-thirds of the libraries in the sample used professional librarians for this purpose. About 15.6% of libraries sampled use paraprofessional support staff for establishment of local name, corporate body and conference headings and authority records; about 65% of libraries sampled use professional librarians for this work. About 15.6% use paraprofessional support staff routinely for establishment of local subject and geographic headings and authority records; more than 67.5% of libraries sampled use professional librarians for this purpose.

Gains & Losses in the Number of Cataloging Positions The libraries in the sample gained a mean of 0.13 positions in support staff cataloging over the past five years. Most gains were incurred by public college libraries. The libraries in the sample gained a mean of 0.23 professional librarian positions in cataloging over the past five years. 46.67% of libraries in the sample participate in library school student mentoring or internships, or the recruitment of existing staff and student workers into the cataloging profession. The mean number of librarians in mostly cataloging functions that are likely to retire over the next five years was a mean of only 0.27. The figure was nearly 0.6 for colleges with more than 10,000 students. The situation was somewhat more serious with paraprofessional staff mostly performing cataloging functions; mean loss of this kind of personnel over the next five years is expected to be 0.65 per library. Public colleges were expected to lose more than one employee each in this area over the next five years. More than 70% of the libraries in the sample say that their catalogers have salary levels that are comparable to those of public service librarians at their institutions. More than 94% of libraries at colleges with more than 10,000 students said this, while only half of community college librarians overall said so.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

43

Appealing for Cataloging Budgetary Support from Library Administration We asked the libraries in the sample about a range of tactics to appeal for cataloging budgetary and staff support from the library administration. The first simple tactic that we asked about was: the articulation of a vision of departmental excellence to library administration and others. Surprisingly, only about 43% of survey participants did this. Research universities led the way; close to 78% of them did this. The next was: Creating and articulating new departmental services and work products to library administration and others. Nearly 56% did so, and the tendency to do so rose with college size. The next was: Creating and articulating new departmental workflows that promote service improvements and integration. More than 58% did so, and, once again, the tendency to do so rose with college size. All of the research libraries in the sample did so. The next tactic was: Marketing and branding cataloging and metadata services and personnel via outreach, newsletters and presentations. Only 9.1% of the cataloging departments in the sample did this, mostly public colleges, many of which were research universities. About 19.5% of the libraries sampled employed none of the tactics previously mentioned.

Work Rate Completion Most cataloging departments say that they do not specify quotas, yet many do apparently give some quantitative guidelines as to what is expected. Some reading between the lines is required here. Academic library cataloging departments prefer not to think of themselves as factories and adopting actual quotas may seem like compelling professional employees to punch a timecard when they enter and exit. So, while there is no timecard punching, there are other, perhaps less intrusive ways to measure output. 29.7% of the libraries in the sample have technical services areas that track turnaround time from Acquisitions receipt to Cataloging to shelf-ready distribution. Only 2.82% of the cataloging agencies in the sample said that cataloger staff work product quotas are very useful and only 25.35% said that they were somewhat useful. Nearly half said that they were misleading or that they detract from quality. All of the colleges that thought of quotas as very useful were community or 4-year primarily B.A. granting institutions. 22.54% of the libraries in the sample find it useful to measure turnaround time from receipt in Cataloging to ready for shelf and more than 63% found it useful to measure this. Virtually all types of libraries thought of this as very useful or useful, though only 6.67% of community colleges found this very useful.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

44

Use of Cataloging Quality Indicators Error rates per bibliographic record are considered a very useful quality indicator by 31.43% of the libraries in the sample and useful by 55.7% of the sample. The completeness of the bibliographic record was considered a very useful quality indicator by nearly 41% of those sampled and useful by 52.11% of them. The indicator of error rates per authority record was considered very useful by 21.54% of participants and useful by 52.31%. The indicator of error rate per holding record was considered a very useful quality indicator for 37.31% of those sampled and somewhat useful by nearly 47.8%. 29.41% found the indicator of error rate per physical processing a very useful quality indicator, while close to 56% considered it a somewhat useful indicator. Patron or staff complaints as a quality indicator was considered very useful by 30% and somewhat useful by 55.71% of survey participants. Patron or staff commendation was considered a very useful quality indicator by about 34.3% of those sampled and somewhat useful by 55.71%. Support or accomplishment of departmental or library goals was considered by far the most useful indicator of quality in cataloging work; close to 62% of those sampled considered this a very useful quality indicator.

Outsourcing of Cataloging Functions 41.56% of the libraries in the sample outsource authority control, obtaining new and updated authority records. Half of all public colleges and more than 61% of colleges with more than 10,000 students do so. Close to 30% of survey participants outsource the acquisition of updated headings and bibliographic records; more than 44% of research universities do so. Also, close to 30% obtain new bibliographic records through outsourcing. Nearly three times as many public as private colleges do so. Only 2.6% of colleges in the sample outsource item records and inventory and most of these were research universities. 11.69% of the libraries in the sample outsource barcoding. More than 44% of research universities did so. 14.29% of the libraries in the sample use outsourcing to add table of contents notes and 10.39% outsourced the obtaining of book reviews. 14.29% obtained book jacket information through outsourcing, and 3.9% outsourced the acquisition of continuing resources (in print). About 15.6% of the libraries in the sample outsource the cataloging of e-journals; close to 28% of research universities do so. 28.57% outsource the cataloging of e-books; 39.5% of public colleges do so, while only about 18% of private college libraries do. Only 1.3% of survey participants outsource the cataloging of AV formats. 3.9% of libraries sampled outsource the cataloging of foreign language resources for which they have no in-house expertise. Most of these libraries had more than 10,000 students and were all were either Ph.D. level or research universities.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

45

2.6% of the libraries in the sample outsource the cataloging of other digital formats not previously cited. 5.19% outsource materials in cataloging backlog. All of these were public colleges and most had more than 10,000 students. 3.9% of the libraries in the sample outsource all of their cataloging. All of these were public community colleges, of which 17.65% outsourced all cataloging. Close to 43% of the libraries in the sample do no outsourcing of cataloging functions at all. More than 61.5% of all private colleges in the sample do not outsource any aspect of cataloging. In general, the larger the college in terms of attendance, the more likely it is to outsource some aspect of cataloging. Only 11.11% of colleges with attendance greater than 10,000 do no outsourcing of cataloging functions at all.

Reviewing and Editing Records 20.78% of libraries in the sample use MarcEdit or other MARC editor to preview records and globally edit to local standards prior to loading. In general, the more complex the degree offered by the college sampled, the higher the likelihood that the college would use MarcEdit or other MARC editor to preview records and globally edit to local standards prior to loading. 24.68% of the colleges in the sample use a Local integrated system to review loaded records and globally edit to local standards; once again, the tendency to do this increased with the complexity of the degree offered. Nearly 24.7% of the libraries in the sample spot check vendor records whenever complete review isn’t possible. 15.6% of those sampled always spot check vendor records; this was most commonly done by colleges with more than 10,000 students. 17% of college in the sample performed no or minimal review.

Cataloging Conference Attendance and Use of Continuing Education in Cataloging Close to 16% of the cataloging agencies in the sample have attended the MOUG, Music Online Users Group, and 1.3% have attended a Public Library Association cataloging-related meeting. A shade more than 9% have attended a conference or taken a continuing education course from the Bibliographic Center for Research, while 3.9% have done so for the Dublin Core Metadata Initiative. 9.1% of survey participants have attended a conference or taken a continuing education class from AMIGOS Library Services Training, while about 7.8% have done so for the Illinois Library Information Network and 3.9% for the Indiana Cooperative Library Services Authority. The same percentage have used the Missouri Library Network Corporation and 5.19% have used the MINITEX Library Information Network Training. 2.6% have used the Michigan Library Consortium, while 7.8% have used services of NELINET and 9.1% have used MARC of Quality. 15.6% of those sampled have used PALINET, while 6.5% have used OHIONET; the same percentage of those surveyed have used Metadata for You and Me for training purposes. About 11.7% of the cataloging agencies sampled used conferences or continuing education

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

46

courses from state libraries for cataloging training. Nearly 43% of the cataloging agencies sampled used conferences or courses from state library associations for cataloging training. 5.2% of the cataloging agencies in the sample have used OCLC Eastern training, and 7.8% have used its Western Service Center Training, while 3.9% have used ACRL’s Rare Books and Manuscripts Cataloging. About 24.7% of the cataloging agencies surveyed have used the Serials Cataloging Cooperative Training Program; use increases with the size of the college. More than 44% of libraries in colleges with more than 10,000 students have used it, as have about 77.8% of research universities in the sample. 10.4% of the libraries in the sample have used the Cooperative Cataloging Training of ALCTS or the Program for Cooperative Cataloging. 6.49% have gone to cataloging-related conferences or taken continuing education courses from the National Information Standards Organization. Only 1.3% of those sampled have done the same with Cataloging for the 21st Century of ALCTS and the Library of Congress. 5.2% of the libraries in the sample attended a conference or took a continuing education course in cataloging of the World Library and Information Congress; all were private colleges, primarily 4-year degree granting.

Rating the Current State of Cataloging Training We asked the libraries in the sample to rate the overall effectiveness of the state of current training and continuing education in cataloging. We gave them four choices: poor, average, good and excellent. About 17% characterized the situation as poor, 66.1% as average, 13.56% as good and 3.4% as excellent. No public college characterized it as excellent, though 20% characterized it as good. Half of all research universities characterized the situation as poor and none characterized it as excellent. On the issue of assessing the effectiveness of the overall state of the ease of identifying or finding pertinent training events, only 6% of participants said that this situation was excellent and 36.4% said it was good. Community colleges seem to be having a particularly difficult time in finding pertinent training events. On the availability of training dates, only 1.47% described the situation as excellent and 35.3% described it as good. On the general usefulness of cataloging training content, 14.7% considered it excellent and 39.7% considered the situation good. The organization of the curriculum was considered excellent by 10.6% and good by 48.5% of participants. More than 61% of survey participants said that in general the handouts that they received in training sessions were excellent or good. Less than a third characterized the situation regarding registration fees for conferences and continuing education in cataloging as either good or excellent and more than 16% regarded the situation as poor. The situation regarding the location of training was regarded as good or excellent by less than 28% of survey participants and 17.65% considered this situation as poor.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

47

The expertise of trainers was not a problem: Nearly 75% considered their trainers to be good or excellent and more than 69% considered the performance of their cataloging trainers to be good or excellent.

Rating the Preparedness of New Academic Library Hires in Cataloging We asked the libraries in the sample to rank the preparedness of recent library hires in a range of cataloging and metadata competencies. They were given four choices: not at all prepared, minimally prepared, prepared, and well prepared. The first competency was in classification systems and only 12.68% said that recent hires were well prepared, and 38% said that they were prepared. Public college libraries were more than seven times as likely as private college libraries to find recent hires not prepared at all in classification system competency. In subject genre/thesauri systems, only 11.27% said that recent hires were well prepared and close to 24% said that they were prepared. In Genre Analysis Principles, rules and tools, less than 10% were well prepared and close to 24% considered their recent hires prepared. In Java and PERL script applications none were well prepared and only 5.88% prepared; nearly two- thirds were not prepared at all. In Cataloging Rules & Tools, 19.72% considered their recent hires well prepared and 31% prepared. In Information Technology and social behavior in the organizational context, 13.85% considered their recent hires well prepared, 39.23% prepared. For Metadata standards for digital initiatives no new hires were considered well prepared, and only 14.5% were considered prepared. In abstracting and indexing, 1.49% of survey participants considered their new hires well prepared, and 11.94% prepared. In electronic delivery of services, about 9% said their new hires were well prepared and close to 30% prepared. Only 5.63% of libraries in the sample felt that their new hires in cataloging were well prepared for work in technical services, and only 38% considered them prepared. Public college libraries were especially likely to find new hires ill-prepared for technical services. In general, colleges with larger student bodies were more likely to find new hires insufficiently prepared for work in technical services. In web and local network system administration and management, only 4.41 considered their new hires well prepared; 19.12% prepared. For traditional books, results were better: 25.71% well prepared and 35.71% prepared. For non-book digital resources, 11.27% were considered well prepared and 21.13% prepared. For continuing resources, less than 25% were considered well prepared or prepared and for legal materials only a shade more than 8% were either prepared or well prepared. In special collections areas such as music, rare materials, archives less than 15% were prepared or well prepared. Preparedness was lower than this for XML or XLST and in the economics and metrics of information.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

48

In discovery tools and applications only 3.1% were well prepared and 24.6% prepared. Even in authority control those considered well prepared were only 11.6% and prepared 24.64%. Only 5.8% said that new cataloging hires were well prepared in International MARC Bibliographic, Authority and Holdings Standards; 34.8% were prepared. None had new cataloging hires that they considered well prepared in data modeling, warehousing and mining and only 7.7% had new hires that they considered prepared. Results were only slightly better for competencies in information system analysis and were worse in programming languages and applications and in relational database design. In OCLC systems and services, 35.2% had hires that they considered prepared and 11.27% had hires that they considered well prepared. In digital libraries and collections, close to 25% had hires that they considered prepared or well prepared.

Participate in college governance and professional organizations. 10. Perform other duties as assigned. The Library Technician I is a generic position. The job description follows: LIBRARY TECHNICIAN I DEFINITION: Performs a wide variety of independent, responsible, and technical tasks related to the acquisitions, cataloging, processing, and/or circulation and distribution of library materials. Position may require working evening and/or weekend shifts. DISTINGUISHING CHARACTERISTICS: The Assistant Library Technician is an entry level position. This position assists in routine library-related tasks under close supervision. The Library Technician I performs a variety of technical duties which requires a general knowledge of library principles, practices and procedures. This position requires previous library experience and knowledge of operating procedures, processes, policies, references, and online utilities used in the public and technical services units of a library. The Library Technician I is responsible for the efficient and effective completion of tasks. The Library Technician II performs broader, more complex specialized and technical duties which require a comprehensive knowledge of library principles, practices, and procedures. The Library Technician II position requires the ability to apply functional area policies and procedures, follow precedent and identify problems and recommend solutions in meeting goals and objectives. SUPERVISION RECEIVED AND EXERCISED: General supervision is provided by a Library Manager and/or a Librarian. Higher level supervision is provided by the Associate Dean of Library and Learning Resources Assignments may require the supervision of student staff. EXAMPLES OF DUTIES: Assists in collection maintenance according to established library principles and procedures, including: identifying materials for rebinding, repair or replacement; organizing and supervising shelving; shelf-reading, shifting, inventory and signage. Maintains financial records of fines, fees, and patron delinquencies; secures collected monies and makes proper deposits. Inputs and retrieves data from the library automated system and other library and campus databases. Assists with the organization and operation of circulation and inventory activities and projects. Assists with the operation, maintenance and repair of library and office equipment available to the public including typewriters, calculators, personal computers, photocopies, and microform reader-printers. Assists in cataloging new materials; searching and retrieving preliminary MARC – format records from OCLC or other bibliographic source; editing records; assigning classification numbers, verifying proper subject headings. Trains student assistants and other personnel assigned. Applies and interprets library policies and procedures and participates in the planning and development of policies and procedures. Provides general library information in person and over the telephone. Processes, organizes, and circulates all materials, including reserve items, periodicals, non-print materials, and books using Dewey Decimal classification, Anglo American cataloging rules, Library of Congress Subject Heading and local standards. Participates in the compilation of daily, monthly, and yearly statistics. Orders and receives a wide variety of library materials including books, pamphlets, serials, and non-print items and notifies requestors of availability of materials. Receives and edits multi-format online bibliographic records and verifies bibliographic citations necessary for ordering materials using the online database, the online catalog, and other bibliographic sources. Prepares purchase orders, initiate payment authorization, selects jobbers and/or publishers, place orders, receives shipments, and responds to inquiries and complaints regarding materials received. 43. The Metadata Librarian reports to the Metadata Group Manager and coordinates metadata and data-entry operations. The Metadata Librarian must have a professional-level understanding of all CLS information discovery and access tools. Currently, this includes the library catalog, the repositories in the Caltech CODA, the Metalib KnowledgeBase, and the SFX KnowledgeBase. However, the number of repositories under development is growing fast, and the Metadata Librarian is expected to be able to handle multiple projects simultaneously. The Metadata Librarian coordinates the work of staff members distributed in various CLS departments and is responsible for quality control of the metadata produced in these projects. The Metadata Librarian must be able to use and to bring to Caltech the latest available tools to find, manipulate, and preserve digital information. The associated activities are: A. Develop an In- House Expertise on Metadata; B. Design and Recommend New Administrative Processes for the Maintenance of CLS information discovery and access tools; C. Develop an Educational Program in Metadata Standards and Best Practices; D. Participate in Outside Professional Activities; E. Conduct Administrative Activities; F. Chapter 3: Work Rate Completion Does your cataloging agency have cataloging quotas, and please explain why, or why not? 1. No. The professional catalogers must teach and work on the reference desk. Paraprofessionals must integrate many formats into their workflow so no set quotas. 2. No quotas for cataloging, however new staff are told how many items the average cataloger should be able to catalog when they are fully trained. New staff who consistently fall below the standard during their probationary period are given quotas/benchmarks to encourage productivity. Retention decisions take into consideration the new staff member's ability to keep up in a production oriented environment. 3. No, the overall environment is very informal because of the small environment. 4. No, but each person is given target quantities to aim for based on past production and library needs. If you spell out an expectation, it encourages productivity. Without it, production quantity tends to decline. 5. No--It has not been an issue. This may be because the director does not have a technical services background and doesn't know enough about what we do to insist on quotas. We aren't going to change that situation. 6. No, material is too variable 7. No, too difficult to police, and there is a Union involved. 8. A cataloger (professional or paraprofessional) may on occasion be assigned a quota for a specified period if s/he is not performing up to par. 9. No. 10. No. Majority of cataloging and processing for new purchases goes through vendor. This leaves more complex cataloging for employees; the efforts involved in cataloging such resources are, by their nature, not easily quantifiable. 11. No. We are a small university in which all librarians do reference, collection development, and instruction in addition to their primary responsibility, so quotas really don't make sense in this environment of extreme teamwork and small staff (5 librarians, 6 support staff). I catalog as I can, given my oversight of cataloging, acquisitions, periodicals, and branch music library operations, plus the additional responsibilities mentioned above. One staff member is an FT cataloger; film cataloging is done by another staff member whose primary responsibilities are in marketing and PR, and who also does reference and instruction; and theses, gov docs, and special collections materials are cataloged by our Special Collections Librarian, who also does general reference. Quotas could only possibly apply to our FT staff cataloger, but she doesn't need them in order to stay productive and motivated. 12. New materials should be cataloged within 5 days. There are no other quotas. 13. No.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

120

14. We do not use quotas per se. Rather, a range of expected times necessary to catalog are given for different types of materials and cataloging and staff are generally expected to work within the guidelines. 15. As long as the book trucks are not piling up we assume the pace is acceptable. 16. No -- we're a small operation. We do what needs to be done in a timely fashion. 17. Not generally, though a quota may be imposed by a supervisor if a staff member's statistics show an unjustified dip in production. My experience with cataloging quotas is that they create a sense of mistrust among staff, and may actually make some employees more prone to committing errors. 18. No - work is current, based on funding for new purchases, otherwise I stay busy with archives work. 19. No quotas, but we are required to document the amount of copy cat, original cat, and authority edits done. 20. No. With such a small staff, it would be obvious if someone was not working as expected. 21. No. I will soon be joining a NACO funnel project for Florida, but currently I have no quotas. 22. No since my job covers many areas. There are weeks when teaching in liaison areas takes the full week. The rest of the staff gets the job done. 23. No quotas, but we do have guidelines for moving things through (LC copy=3 weeks max, member & original = 3 months max). 24. Not explicit quotas but a strong culture of no back logs. 25. No — amount of material to be catalogued is largely dependent on amount purchased or donated by faculty. 26. We do not have productivity quotas because we do not have a backlog. We do have standards for accuracy. 27. No. 28. No, we do not have quotas, only time goals. 29. No. We don't have a backlog of purchased materials (there is a small one of donations, which are lower priority), so keeping up with incoming materials is a sufficient measure of productivity. 30. No. Department is small and materials arrive at a slow and steady pace. Rarely if ever have a backlog of materials. 31. No, there is currently no evidence of need. 32. No quotas, but for those who need numeric goals, because cataloging numbers are low, we set a goal to shoot for.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

121

33. No 34. No, we are governed by collective bargaining and do not how people accountable, although that is beginning to change. 35. We have a vague quota or goal, but it is not enforced in performance reviews. 36. No - Our catalogers are very good and we have no backlog so there is no reason to set quotas. 37. Not sure what you mean, but I don't think so. If I understand the concept, we have not felt a need for them up to now. 38. No - we just try our best to get the work done. Quotas would be unfair due to the variations in workflow required by reference and instruction responsibilities. 39. No, we catalog as materials are acquired. This means heavy and light workload, depending on budget, which has been abysmal. 40. No. We're too small. 41. No, we take care of whatever acquisitions are ordered or donated. We're a small library for a small school, but we are affiliated with OhioLINK. 42. There are no cataloging quotas. This is a medium-size library, and there is no cataloging backlog. 43. No; expectation is for no item to remain in cataloging for more than 14 days. 44. No. Most others in the library don't understand enough about cataloging to have implemented quotas. They're just happy we don't have a backlog. 45. No. We keep statistics about our output, but we don't have quotas. 46. Since we pay per record, the time spent is irrelevant. Catalogers determine the number of batches they receive, but are responsible for getting those completed by a deadline. 47. Only based on time. We operate 2 locations as OPL's, so time determines how our cataloguing quota. 48. No, because I do not ask for them. 49. No. As a small library there are times when we are not able to purchase, consequently cataloging staff work on other projects. Comparisons of expectations for a variety of tasks would be helpful in developing standards for our catalog/tech svs staff. 50. We have "soft" quotas, or expectations of only 200 titles per staff member per month, though this is subject to other projects that staff are working on. 51. No. We're too small--we just catalog whatever comes in.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

122

52. No. Our workflow is "seasonal" as it were, and we rarely have problems with backlogs. Plus, our support staff, being students, are 10-hour-per-week employees with little incentive to meet such quotas anyway. We're don't have the market for the kind of staff we can coerce with penalties for not meeting quotas. 53. No - cataloging is done between other tasks. 54. No specific quotas, but statistics in the form of numbers are considered very, very important by the Dean of Libraries; staff have been let go for appearing unproductive (not necessarily based on an understanding of cataloging). 55. No, we have too many diverse functions that we also perform. 56. We do not have quotas instead we have acceptable processing time lengths--we like a book to "hit the shelves" within 2 weeks of it coming in to the building. 57. Relatively speaking. We only have one cataloguer, so that person is responsible for getting materials on the shelves within a reasonable amount of time. 58. We do not have quotas; don't know why that is. However, it would be difficult to adhere to quotas in the academic library environment, where the workflow varies widely from one month to the next depending upon faculty requests for new materials, number of library instruction classes taught by cataloger, and other fluctuating demands on time. 59. No. We are a small, but very busy library with few materials being acquired at present. We are also lacking 3 positions, 1 professional and 2 para-professional, leaving a staff of 5 with only 2-3 student workers. A great amount of our time is spent at the circulation/reference desk due to the staffing situation. We also have to cover nights as well as Saturday hours. The priority in this library is the front/circulation/reference desk. 60. No. 62. No. 63. No, we have one cataloger, all based on workflow. 64. No. 65. No, I've never heard of them! 66. We do not use quotas. We commit to doing our best to get materials out to the shelf within 10 days of receipt in the cataloging unit, and we will catalog and process materials immediately upon demand. Our organization is generally satisfied with our performance. 67. I don't understand what "cataloging agency" refers to, but we do not deal with quotas in any form. 68. No. I just catalog what we purchase, which is a fairly small number of things. 69. We have stats, no quotas. 70. No; work is cyclical - if nothing is ordered have nothing to catalog. Also, have many part- timers involved who have other responsibilities.

71. No, not necessary given cataloging volume. 72. No, due to the dynamic nature of our acquisitions. 73. No. We have 1 staff and 1 librarian. Our budget is small and we have no backlog. No need for quotas.

How does your cataloging department define quality? 1. Providing excellence in access points for cataloging records. 2. Quality is defined by the type of copy and/or workflow staff are working on. For example, records used for retrospective conversion are not upgraded to current standards but if original cataloging is necessary, all current standards are met. 3. The consortium has a minimum requirement of core-level records but higher level records are strongly encouraged. 4. Producing the most accurate cataloging records possible in the shortest amount of time. 5. By catching our mistakes before the items go to the shelves (double-checking our work) and by quickly correcting errors that are found. We want the most complete records possible that will make it likely that students will be able to find materials for their papers and projects. We do a lot of enhancing of bib records. 6. Providing access to the material in a consistent and timely manner, using standards established within the profession. 7. Following National, International, and local standards to the best of our ability while getting items through as quickly as possible. 8. Cataloging to our standards, done quickly. We are collection-rich and staff-poor, so we have learned to balance cataloging with the need to get things to the shelf quickly. We also outsource a lot.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

135

9. Adequate access; almost no errors. 10. The ability we provide users to find and use our resources. 11. Lack of errors affecting access. 12. Quality is best available record selected; processing, call numbers, etc., adhere as closely as possible to established quality guidelines. 13. The demonstration of integrity in the catalog via ease of access, limited duplication and "cleanliness". 14. Records of all sorts should accurately reflect content, descriptions, access points, subject content, and patron accessibility following current library and national standards to enhance retrieval in the library catalog. 15. We don't define quality because leadership is not concerned about it. 16. Accuracy. "Proofread, proofread, proofread." (Edward Lowinsky, University of Chicago). 17. Tough question. Our goal is to provide clear descriptive information, consistent, logical subject analysis and organization, and appropriate linkages to resources both internal and external to the catalog. We have a strong service ethic and are responsive to all inquiries related to the catalog or cataloging in general. If we're not doing that, we have a quality problem. 18. 95% accuracy in work. 19. A MARC record with access points through title, subjects, and names, completed in a reasonable amount of time as determined by the staff and students' need for that item. 20. Efficiency balanced with adherence to national cataloging standards. But service to patrons and staff is the primary goal. 21. Creating accurate and complete cataloging records. 22. Since the entire department is 1 professional (me) and my part-time assistant, we just do our best. We have goals and when we meet those goals, we're happy. We've had to sacrifice some quality (such as authority control) with a switch to a new ILS that is much more cumbersome than our previous system. I like to see bibliographic records that are free from spelling errors, free from punctuation problems, all the correct MARC fields, added contents notes as appropriate, and correct classification. 23. Getting the materials on the shelves and the best possible record describing the material in hand. 24. Full and accurate subject analysis Accuracy in index points Rate of through-put Consistency in following national & international rules & guidelines. 25. Quality consists of both accurate metadata (especially descriptive metadata) and the getting material into the collection quickly.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

136

26. 1) Comprehensive and accurate description of materials received 2) Classification of such materials in such a manner as will be most helpful to our users 3)Timely processing of said materials. 27. We define quality for support staff by percentage error rate in the following: selection or suitability of OCLC record as a match for item cataloged; correcting typographical errors in the following fields: 100, 245, 260, 300, 5XX; making appropriate edits to bibliographic and holdings records; accurate creation of item and holdings records; recognizing cataloging problems and bringing them to the attention of a supervisor. For cataloging librarians: quality is defined by excellent original cataloging based on AACR2 full-level standards; name authority records created to standards set by NACO; effective supervision of support staff, including timely resolution of questions and problems; a reasonable turnaround time for materials so that a backlog is not created or growing; responsiveness to needs of internal and external patrons. completeness, efficiency, responsive to queries and complaints. 28. Item ready on the shelf with accurate, complete cataloging records in system within 1 month of acquisition. 29. Materials cataloged and processed promptly yet carefully. 30. Ease of findability of library materials. 31. Quality is defined as a complete description of a work, including all access points, notes, subjects, classification, in order to provide the online catalog user with the most information possible to determine if this work fits their needs. 32. Useful, complete, and intuitive bibliographic records which enhance our student/faculty/staff library experience. 33. No. 34. Sufficient description. 35. Our catalogers have a very high level work ethic and they take great pride in moving materials out as fast and as accurately as possible. Few complaints would definitely indicate quality and also commendations from faculty and staff. 36. Accuracy of entries and time from arrival of material until it is on the shelf. 37. Conformance with generally accepted standards and usability by patrons in the catalog. 38. Complete bib records with subject classification to match the college's instructional program. 39. Links to online resources when appropriate. Accurate item records. 40. Unfortunately, we are a small and somewhat inexperienced staff who do everything. Quality is finding the best record we can and getting access to the item as quickly as we can. Record perfection is not a goal. 41. Patrons can find our materials will little or no trouble. 42. Adherence to internationally established cataloging codes and locally-established policies/procedures in the description of all library materials.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

137

43. Achieving excellence. 44. I expect very few errors. My paraprofessional understands the importance of accuracy, but is easily distracted. After 4 years on the job, I still must check all books that she processes. The error rate ranges from only a few on a truck to nearly half of a truck. 45. Adherence to standards; participation in statewide network & its standards; professional, experienced catalogers doing the work; no significant backlogs; full use of authority control vendor; using only two well-trained student workers instead of many to do processing; rush cataloging requests handled immediately. 46. Error free records prepared in accordance with AACR2/MARC and local adaptations. Error-free and quality/level of cataloging. 47. Having records with valid subject headings, description and classification. 48. No stated definition. 49. Least number of errors while maintaining high productivity and turnaround time. 50. Can we find the book when we need it? 51. If the record accurately describes the work in hand and effectively directs the searcher to the appropriate content, then it meets our standard of quality. Timeliness is only an issue when faculty spell out expectations for turnaround of requested materials. 52. The call number puts material in the same area as other books on the topic. 53. I define it more strictly than the Dean and Deparmental Chair. Quality means correct application of headings, uniform titles, spelling, accuracy in identifying and applying the title and alternative titles, correct choice of MARC fields, attention to some of the most important fixed fields used in OPAC display, searching, and report making. 54. An item that a faculty person requests, is ordered and received quickly, cataloged promptly with enough points of access to make it accurately and easily found again through our OPAC and a notification sent to the initial requester. The item is processed and shelved accurately. All work done should be prompt and correct. 55. The Library is not a research university, therefore it has little original cataloging to do and quality is defined in terms of materials being accessible to the patron in a timely manner. 56. Getting the items on the shelves in a reasonable amount of time and knowing that items are retrievable easily by Reference librarian and patrons. 57. Ideally, we would like materials to be ready for circulation within 2-4 weeks of receipt by Acquisitions Dept., but this typically is accomplished only for faculty purchases, which get priority over all other materials. 58. There are no local definitions or checks for quality in our cataloging department. 59. Quick turnaround time; accurate holdings in OPAC.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

138

60. Timely, current, correct metadata records 61. Does the catalog work, does it make sense, can students find what they need. 62. Material is available and accessible to patrons and other users of online catalog. 63. Low backlog, excellence of record. 64. Ordering and processing materials accurately and in a timely manner to meet instructor/student needs. 65. Production of products that accurately describe and label resources, conform to standards, and that assist users in finding, selecting, locating, and obtaining information resources within our collection that meet their information needs efficiently. 66. We have not defined quality. 67. Compliance with the applicable state, corporate and institution standards. 68. No errors in bib and item records, or in physical processing tasks, such as labeling 69. Error rate of less than 4 out of 100 new books that I return to cataloger to fix. 70. Currently undefined. 71. Zero defects. 72. Accuracy of the OPAC.

Has your cataloging department ever conducted cost-time-motion studies or data driven analysis for any cataloging workflows? Please explain. 1. No. 2. A time-motion study was done for each of our cataloging workflows: Copy cataloging of PCC- like records, Other copy cataloging, Literature, Art, and Original cataloging to determine benchmarks. 3. No 4. Yes, we determined that over 50% of our output is for AV materials and remotely accessed electronic resources--this resulted in developing some of the positions into specialty jobs--e.g. music cataloger; AV cataloger, Electronic resources cataloger, etc. 5. Not that I know of. 6. Not since I have been here (5 years).

7. No. 8. For a few years I kept detailed spreadsheets on activity and cost, overall and for individual staff, and it included staff costs. Chapter 4: Technology Please explain any difficulties your area has faced in improving and upgrading staff-use of hardware and software technology. 1. None. 2. Staff are interested in learning/using anything that will improve their efficiency especially if it cuts down on repetitive key strokes. 3. None -- we have found that the new web based applications run slower that the old command driven ones reducing overall productivity. Staff adapts easily to changes. 4. Gaining the knowledge to implement upgrades in our technology--we have only one IT person in our library, and he is mostly busy with OPACs. We need more technical instruction tailored to our staff. We honestly don't know what the technology can do for us--or how to implement it. 5. We are still doing way too much with paper! 6. No problems. 7. We're an III site. We needed to migrate from the old text based system to the Windows based system. People found the Windows based system took 'longer' than text based at first, but later admitted that the Windows system is faster. 8. Every ILS upgrade creates some problems. Here's a small problem we're having now: some of our Macro Express macros are not working as well as they used to, but only on some PCs. 9. Mostly we consider new technologies as opportunities. 10. Innovative system sometimes has caused problems (mainly error reports) 11. Paraprofessionals have always used propriety software. Even Connexion and especially Millennium don't always follow Windows protocols. Paraprofessionals are not adept at picking up new software applications. 12. How timely! We had a great spine label printing program that ceased to work when we upgraded to MS 2007 (Access was the main culprit). We want to batch print sheet labels. Millennium can do this with one, and only one type of Gaylord label, which is wasteful, as the sheets contain other types of labels we do not use. Right now we're doing this, but we can only print labels for general collection materials, as Millennium doesn't pull information from the location field of the item record, information that indicates special locations such as Folio or Music. I am planning to call them about this in the next week. If they can't accommodate this need, and given the wastefulness of printing directly from Millennium when batch printing sheet labels, I will probably look into using the third party software Print Wizard. I have already determined that OCLC's printing options don't really fit our needs. 13. Some staff are reluctant to move to upgraded software when the older version is still available.

I don't think it's a good thing to rely on one huge monopoly (OCLC) for metadata and cataloging. If you have outsourced library resources, please briefly explain why you outsourced certain types of library resources. 1. We continue to outsource the cataloging of Chinese, Japanese and Korean materials because we do not have the language expertise to catalog these items. In the past we outsourced the remainder of our Dewey classed items that were still represented by card catalog cards; several "Special Collections" that were only represented by cards in the catalog (e.g., English Short Title); a music score backlog that developed when we did not have a music cataloger. 2. Sometimes we are able to buy records for certain collection sets: such as Net Library, Safari books, etc. and load them in batches. We also use LTI for authority control--importing and updating authority records. 3. Cataloging something like e-books is beyond the scope of our staff. We get MARC records for our Humanities E-Book subscription. 4. We have done cataloging backlogs and foreign languages. Languages were in non-roman script and no one can translate here. When the library lacked a cataloging position, outsourcing was used to get caught up. 5. Does question 25 refer to the items in question 24? If so, we outsource all materials where possible in those ways. We also outsource some special materials once. 6. Most of our new print monographs come through PromptCat/YBP shelf-ready. We outsource original cataloging of difficult languages and formats to TechPro, but will do so less with our new Cataloging Librarian. 7. One project of Arabic gift books (none here knew Arabic).

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

166

8. Insufficient personnel 9. N/A 10. Years ago we tested PromptCat, but got cataloging in-house done faster. We used Marcive for government documents but incorrect records and system overlay issues caused more problems than they were worth. 11. Because our cataloging consortium provides those services. 12. We are piloting the outsourcing of cataloging for firm orders from our primary book vendor. We plan to outsource cataloging of approval plan books. 13. Net library purchased from Solinet, Google Books, and other sources with OCLC records. 14. Not true outsourcing, but we generate brief records for e-journals from data provided by Serials Solutions. We download records for videos from Midwest Tapes rather than from OCLC directly. We obtain records for government documents from Marcive. Most authority control is done in-house, but Marcive provides authority records for new government documents cataloging and also updates (changed, cancelled records) for our complete authority file. 15. We get MARC records for our E-journals from EBSCO because we wanted to provide catalog access to these holding but it was too much work to keep up with locally. 16. Monographs were outsourced because our book budget increased dramatically and we could not handle the increased work with the staff we had. We use vendor records for e-journals and e-books because it's a more effective way of making these materials quickly available in the catalog. 17. Marc records are purchased with large collections of electronic books. 18. Outsourced bib records for e-books and e-journals because it was time and cost effective (records were offered as part of the packages). 19. Sent bib/authority records to Marchive for error auditing. 20. We outsourced some large ebook sets, as there are simply too many ebooks in large collections to individually catalog, or to use one by one via OCLC. We outsourced a section of our books backlog as we did not have the staff to gain control over it, and we wanted to obtain full, high quality cataloging in a quick manner. We have an on-going workflow to send LC cataloging records for books to an authority vendor, and then to receive matching authorities and updated bibliographic records from them for our database. We may utilize YBP's full MARC record service during the selection process, to be later updated and overlaid by PromptCat records when the books are received, with minimal checking to be done on the records. We also may obtain cataloging records for leased books, which would be overlaid by full OCLC records (done in house) upon their purchase. 21. N/A 22. Authorities, not easily accommodated with local system.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

167

23. I don't understand question #25. E-books & e-journals are outsourced if you consider the purchase of vendor records to be a type of outsourcing, like SFX or purchasing records for netLibrary (which I do). But, plain old print monographs are not on your list and we effectively outsource them too through PromptCat. This depends on your definition of outsourcing. 24. Authority control is outsourced and sometimes ebooks come with MARC records. Most library resources are cataloged in-house. 25. Vendor records provide what we need and reduce our workload. 26. MARCIVE supplies barcodes, and brief and full records for our Federal government documents. There is insufficient staff in that particular area of the library to keep up with the flow of information from the government. We have purchased some NetLibrary eBooks, plus the cataloging records for them. It is easier and more cost-efficient to outsource certain groups of materials, particularly those which are non-recurring like the NetLibrary eBooks, than to overburden the cataloging staff. 27. Lack of manpower to manually add info to use free cover services such as Amazon or Library Thing. 28. We will load thousands of e-book records rather than catalog individually due to availability and quality of the records. 29. Academic libraries outsource to us. 30. No knowledge of language of materials. 31. We do very little original cataloging. Most materials have records available in OCLC. 32. This question is a bit misleading. We have outsourced parts of the workflow for certain types of materials, such as e-resources, where we outsource the cataloging, and maintaining holdings data. We have also outsourced record derivation to our book jobber, YBP and have used OCLC TechPro to provide shelf-ready materials from time to time. This was largely an issue of time and staffing. 33. We get the NetLibrary collections, which come pre-cataloged. Entering those records into our catalog is a major effort. 34. We're actually planning to use OCLC's NetLibrary records for our ebook collection. In a sense it's not really "outsourcing" any more than using WorldCat, except that we're doing it in batch from canned records. 35. We tried outsourcing a special collection with OCLC, but the work of outsourcing these vulnerable materials proved more work on our part than just cataloging ourselves. We quit using the vendor after 6 months. 36. None at this point. Previously, we outsourced our book backlog but we were not pleased with the quality of the cataloging. 37. We outsource music scores that require original cataloging because we do not have a music cataloger on staff. 38. N/A

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

168

39. We use OCLC to obtain bibliographic records for all items that do not require original cataloging, and for all authority records. We subscribe to a book rental service from Brodart (McNaughton collection), which includes prepared bibliographic records and physical processing of books. This McNaughton subscription is for only 10 books per month, so represents a tiny fraction of our total cataloging output. 40. Our outsourcing is limited to batch ingest of cataloging records provided by vendors. 41. Limits - number of staff and knowledge of staff - of staff resulted in the outsourcing of authority control. 42. The ability to obtain information at a lower cost. 43. I work with Baker & Taylor and have contracted for them to process items. It was more cost effective to do this than for me to do them myself. 44. We use Serials Solutions 360 Core to help manage our holdings for E-journals and provide our A-Z journal title list. 45. N/A 46. Little original cataloging done here. 47. Convenience -- easy to acquire e-book records as part of purchase.

What are the criteria you use, if any, to analyze and determine the best sources of high quality records for outsourced materials? 1. Availability of suitable records. 2. We can't do comparisons; we take what HEB offers and check carefully. 3. Only done once and used TechPro. 4. Reputation, ability to meet local requirements. 5. OCLC is always top-notch. We have also used a local company, Donohue, for a project; they have an excellent reputation. 6. Literature review; peer consultation. 7. N/A 8. The records would need to meet PCC standards and be deliverable promptly. 9. We don't have a choice. 10. Our vendor uses OCLC. We're limiting record selection to PCC/DLC/UKM only.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

169

11. We expect OCLC to be standardized and check for any problems. 12. Reputation and cost of service providers. 13. Fullness and accuracy, for outsourced print monographs. For e-books, we take what we can get, and edit to match local standards. 14. Professional training and judgment. 15. We review the records using MarcEdit, opening them and taking a look-see for description and access points in various part of the file; We perform the field count report to see field anomalies and determine what the problems are and whether we can fix them or not; Records must have an LC call number and correct LCSH; We compare them to print records (for e-book cataloging) on OCLC to see how they compare to full cataloging records available 16. N/A 17. Sample record evaluation using tools like MarcEdit. It depends on the record set & how badly we need it & how many records are included. If there are 35,000 records (ie. an e-book set) and there is no way to catalog them by hand, we do not have very many criteria. For materials that we could do ourselves, then we look at the fullness of the records, including subject analysis & classification. 18. Company reputation and procedures. 19. We obtain them from the vendor from whom we obtain the material - e.g. Netlibrary, Cenage/Gale, Credo. 20. Accuracy and completeness of records; adherence of cataloging copy to AACR2, etc.; ease of loading into local integrated library system. 21. None. 22. Having the cataloging department head analyze samples of the records to determine if they are acceptable. 23. While we often catalogue all acquistions for a small spceical library, for an academic library it would be unusual materials, e.g., old engravings, scores, CDs, remote electronic resources. 24. We make a huge analysis of outsourcing vendors, ask them questions, ask around to peers. 25. Original source of the record, LC preferred. 26. We look at sample records of all sources of MARC data before purchasing and loading those records. In cases where cataloging may be subpar, unless there is an alternate source, we have to go with the less optimal data until we have the time to upgrade later. 27. Our consortium requires OCLC standard records. Non-OCLC records are only allowed under special circumstances (i.e. electronic vendors who allow their records to be used in a global environment. So, to respond to the question more directly, we do not actively solicit records outside of the OCLC environment.

28. It has to be easy for us to integrate the outsourced records into our catalog; cataloging needs to be as close to the best one can find in OCLC as possible. 29. N/A 30. We look at the expertise required to catalog the materials (language, type of material) and then correlate that to the expertise of individuals on staff. 31. N/A 32. Not applicable. We take whatever records they provide, then enhance on our own if needed. 33. Must be: MARC standard; must be complete level record. 34. Trials of providers, recommendations from others. 35. Not applicable. 36. Our consortium handles outsourced Authority control. We do no outsourcing locally. 37. N/A 38. Price and ease of use.

What, if any, specific metadata training does your agency need in order to provide an adequate knowledge base for metadata librarians? 1. Hands-on workshops 2. Variety of standards beyond Dublin Core and practical application of those standards.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

211

3. Not familiar enough to say, but at least a minimal level in the currently used metadata schemas. 4. It's pretty easy to get general training in the most popular standards, but applying them locally depends upon need and appropriate software. While we use Dublin Core for our institutional repository, we have developed other (Oracle) databases using local standards, with an eye toward being able to crosswalk them to Dublin Core. I think once we begin digitizing LIBRARY collections we will have a greater need for training in the various standards. 5. We use only MARC here. 6. Parameters and best practices for finding aids for special collection items. 7. MARCXML, MODS 8. Why are we so rule orientated when keyword search is most used? 9. Advanced serials training 10. Actual hands-on training. Theory is fine, but I want to see the practical application of metadata. I want to see what it means to create metadata for different types of information (digital sound clips, photos that have been digitized, film reels, etc.). 11. Training needs to be <much> more specific; it would be very helpful to have someone come to our institution, help us to identify what materials we have that would benefit from being made available in specific metadata formats, and then "walking us through” the process 12. None. 13. AACR2r, MARC, LOC Authorities, LCSH. 14. I'd like to see a core list of metadata standards and for what situations, what agency-types, they might apply. I'd like to know of a standard for digital collections and institutional repositories, and how it is being applied in various libraries, agencies. I'd like to see a set of core competencies for metadata librarians which also come with a core set of tools and practices in use. 15. Don't know. 16. I have theoretical understanding of how metadata works. Not sure how to transition or begin from nothing. Not sure how this would incorporate into our local catalog. 17. hands-on, concrete training. Also, why is SOLINET not listed in question in question 29/30? We use SOLINET quite a bit. And 29/30 are repeats. 18. You name it. 19. We need the money for digitization projects and for metadata tools first. 20. We need to start from the ground up. 21. Anything we can get our hands on. We basically have little to no knowledge of how to use Dublin Core, EAD, TEI, etc. There are too few faculty/staff to allow time for training and

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

212

implementation of these formats. This kind of thing gets short shrift because the normal daily work, which supports the mission of the institution, must be done first. 22. ? 23. I've only been out of library school for 8 years, but I had no idea RDA was supposed to replace AACR2 until this spring! I am struggling to quickly learn all I can about RDA, FRBR, FRAD, and other cataloging alphabet soups that weren't taught in library school and have been flying well below my radar.
