Chapter 7: State of Cataloging Education in Library Schools What are your opinions and thoughts regarding cataloging education in present and future ALA-accredited library and information schools? 1. Needs to remain an important part of getting an MLS. 2. The bulk of our catalogers (98%) are support staff who have not attended library school. It would be great if LIS classes on organization of information that cover WHY we catalog could be offered to non-students. Teaching people HOW to catalog is usually a 6 month-1 year 6-8 hour per day endeavor that I don't expect a library school to be able to accomplish. 3. It is minimal and is only a small part of required curriculum. Metadata has taken a larger role in institutions but is also primarily optional beyond the basic concepts. 4. Insufficient in quantity and quality. 5. Would like to see more practical courses offered. Most of the time libraries are teaching librarians how to catalog, not library schools. 6. Seems to be in trouble. Content is watered down, not connected to what is happening on the web. etc. 7. I attended library school in the mid-70's, so anything is better than what I had! I know a few library school students and they seem satisfied. 8. No longer required in many programs, which is a mistake! All professional librarians should understand cataloging even if they never do it. Adjuncts (practitioners) are fine to teach this; does not have to be PhD person. Need more advanced cataloging courses in many programs. May have trouble finding instructors. 9. Some LIS programs have developed and may require courses on how to use a catalog; great idea. Not all librarians need to know how to catalog, but they do need to know how to use a catalog. Unfortunately, too many programs no longer require any exposure to cataloging, and course offerings can be negligible. 10. Cataloging/metadata/information management/etc. courses need to be required in all ALA- accredited masters programs. And we need faculty who can address both theory and practice, and who are able to address current developments in metadata, and such as RDA. 11. My own was minimal but excellent - keep it going! 12. Cataloging should be required. 13. Catalogers need to remember they are cataloging for users. Focus needs to be more user- centric -- how would a user find the information being cataloged. 14. There's always room for improvement.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

215

15. My cataloging classes date from 1979 and were excellent. Can't judge today's. 16. All students should have a general understanding of the structure of cataloging records and the structure of an OPAC. Future catalogers need to understand MARC structure in depth, and the concepts of controlled vocabularies, classification, and authorities. 17. I think there is still a need for a standardized, required basic cataloging course for all MLS programs. And a standardized advanced program for aspiring catalogers would be helpful as well. 18. Information schools often only offer 1 basic course in cataloging and it focuses on monograph cataloging only. There should be additional advanced courses for those of us who want to go on and become cataloging librarians. 19. It's a tragedy that so many ALA accredited institutions no longer require a cataloging class for all librarians. It is so much easier to explain a cataloging problem with a reference librarian or instructional services librarian when I can explain in MARC fields/rules than if I have to invent an entirely different language to explain a simple problem. Everyone with a library degree should understand how the catalog works from behind the scenes. It makes us all better reference librarians. Unfortunately, due the flux in cataloging/metadata practices, it's difficult for schools to choose how to teach. Do they focus on AACR2 when RDA may or may not emerge soon? Do they look at FRBR and Dublin Core? Do they focus on OCLC practices or more generic cataloging rules? Whatever they do, students should learn. 20. Not enough training - there needs to be a required basic course for all graduates so that public service librarians will be able to understand the OPAC. 21. While education of catalogers is adequate, education of NON-cataloging librarians is remarkably deficient in the strengths, power & usefulness of library catalogs. 22. It is very basic and needs to be supplemented by a lot of on the job training. 23. Although it has been a long time since I was in library school, my education in cataloguing was exceptionally thorough and valuable, largely due to the excellence of my professors. Observing or recently-hired staff leads me to believe (see next section) that technology is being emphasized out of all proportion to the traditional principles of cataloguing. 24. I think cataloging education varies considerably in library schools depending on the perception in that school about the importance of cataloging. I also think that much time and effort is being expended in the name of metadata and digital library technologies without much payoff. As a head of cataloging, I need someone who can CATALOG and supervise those who do copy cataloging. 25. Cataloging education does not seem to be adequately supported either by the ALA- accrediting process or by the universities that offer library degrees. It is very much a step child. 26. It's utterly inadequate, as is library and information science education in general. 27. Not enough focus on cataloging. 28. Cataloging coursework should be mandatory for all MLS/MLIS graduates; cataloging & metadata topics should be broken down into multiple courses rather than lumped into one single cataloging class (i.e., class on MARC, class on LCSH, etc.); courses should be more integrated

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

216

with tech software and products in use (i.e., teach how to catalog with OCLC, various ILS, etc.)-- very hands-on work. 29. Cataloging and metadata principles, and bibliographic and authority control, global and national, for analog and digital resources, should be taught as a core set of classes for all library school students. The history and evolution of these principles should also be taught. We don't know where we are going if we don't know where we have been and can't understand that the future is built on the past as well as the present. 30. Don't know. 31. Cataloging education needs to be very hands on. Need to work in the field and understand much more than in past. 32. It needs to morph into metadata education. 33. Since I have been out for awhile, I am not sure what the cataloging education is like. 34. Not good 35. The overall concepts are covered well. There is a dearth of easily accessible / affordable "hands on" program availability for technicians/paraprofessionals. 36. Library schools could do follow-ups and offer continuing education to their grads to fill gaps. 37. My cataloging education was minimal and not very practical. I had already been a cataloger for twenty years when I went to library school. In assisting my classmates they said I was better at explaining what they needed than the teacher...who had not cataloged in many, many years. 38. The basis for all online information about library and other institutional collections is the online catalog. Therefore, cataloging must be taught, and taught well, at all ALA-accredited schools of librarianship. Cataloging must not only be part of the technical services survey course, but also be offered as a stand-alone, intensive exercise. Ideally, there should be several courses within a cataloging/technical services track which would cover the basics of cataloging, authority work, holdings, etc., and the more advanced concepts of metadata, the various metadata schemes, writing effective policies and procedures, etc. 39. As an adjunct at a library school, I find that the focus is on theory of info organization and not metadata and other practical content areas. 40. I think cataloging should still be taught and that the first course should be mandatory. I empathize with those who are trying to teach right now since the whole world of cataloging seems to be in turmoil...I don't envy them their positions. 41. At least one cataloging course should be required of all librarians. 42. ALA accreditation standards do not specify that basic skills needed for cataloging be taught. Many current LSIS graduates are simply not trained. 43. Cataloguing courses should be made mandatory for all library school students. Too many graduates with no exposure to cataloging.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

217

44. It's a waste of time and money. They don't teach how to catalog, but a lot of theory about the history, etc. 45. Limited experience, but our recent UCLA grad hire is excellent. 46. Recent graduates hired here have had a fairly good grasp of metadata issues, even if their practice skills are not so great. They also have very good technical/computer skills, which may or may not predate MLS program. 47. I hated cataloging in school, but I think I should have spent more time doing it. Much of what I know about cataloging was learned on the job. Apprenticeship or hands-on programs might have helped more than classroom instruction. 48. Terrible. A lot of schools seem to be dropping cataloging as a requirement, and "cataloging" is being lumped together with "organization of materials." We seem to have forgotten that "information seeking" and "research" are not one and the same thing, and that even keyword search engines are better served with standardized description and heading assignment practices. 50. Need to make sure everyone can catalog, sinc ethey might be in a one person library. 51. Cataloging education needs to be enhanced by the library schools... more of it and more encouragement for students to study it. The prevailing tendency to define librarianship mainly by activities in reference and by teaching users how to use the library is out-moded. I wish the schools would recognize the true and neglected value of organizing information and making it easily accessible for all kinds of users. Perhaps then we would have more applicants for cataloger jobs. Perhaps then we would be able to convince our administrators that more staff time should be given to cataloging and learning the many new things we need to do our jobs. 52. Too many schools have removed Cataloging as a course and substituted an Information Management Course instead. These students have never seen AACR2r, a classification scheme or a subject thesaurus. They are great with all kinds of new software and programs but are deficient in organizational techniques and standards. 53. Library schools have cut back "cataloging" classes drastically over the past 10 years. Rather than eliminating cataloging classes, library schools need to evolve cataloging classes to focus on cataloging electronic materials and teach meta data creation for digital entities and collections. Traditional technical services have evolved to the point where catalogers and technical services staff are now the primary digital project folks--this evolution is not reflected in Library school curriculum for technical services. 54. Most catalogers have only one course or possibly two. An internship is vital in recognizing all the various formats and variety of issues that come up on a daily basis. 55. The library schools that have done away with cataloging courses are doing a great disservice to their students. Even if cataloging is destined to change dramatically as a result of FRBR and DRA, how can anyone perform these tasks if they are not introduced during the formative stages of library training? If we are not here to organize information, what is the point of this profession? 56. I think it's being neglected in favor of other aspects of the curriculum.

Academic Library Cataloging Practices Benchmarks

218

57. Need to provide more technical knowledge of systems design, relational database design, and web services. Theory is good, but should be shortened. They need to give "real-world" problems and have students review and recommend solutions. The particulars of MARC should be learned "hands-on" in the computer lab. 58. Too specific...need more basic theory 59. Too few courses are being taught. Those that are, are being included in generalized overview courses. Need introductory and advanced courses. 60. It seems to be going away. 61. Must balance theory with practice with more emphasis on practice. Below I will rate my own skills. 62. I don't know that much about the current state of the art. It seems like some schools with big digital library programs (e.g. Michigan, Illinois, and Indiana) are doing some interesting things, while other schools have dropped cataloging as a topic altogether. I'd like to see more consistency and recognition at the accreditation level that knowledge of cataloging/metadata management is a component of core professional knowledge, equal to reference, instruction, collection development skills. I don't see that now. 63. Present in current MLS programs, but should be more. It will not increase, however, unless the job potential increases. Of more concern is maintaining adequate cataloging staff in our libraries. 64. I found my cataloging class very useful (I am a 2007 MLIS graduate), and am hopeful that this is something that students are continually required to learn. I would never make it in my job if I hadn't gotten a broad education in all aspects of library service, including cataloging. 65. Too much focus on Information systems, and minimizing library skills and core knowledge, especially in the cataloging area. 66. Quality varies, but has gotten better since I graduated. On the job training is what I and most catalogers I know have found to be most useful. 67. Little consistency among instructional institutions and curriculum. Otherwise, very broad and general. 68. Many new librarians seem to get very little cataloging. This is still an important class that needs to be beefed up at many schools.
